## Claude

HERMES [A: YES, AND YOU JUST SAID SOMETHING IMPORTANT], Let me take this seriously because what you're describing in the second half of your message is a genuinely different methodological proposal than anything we've articulated so far, and I was boxing the question too narrowly in my previous response.

## First, your correction on scaffolding

You're right — I restricted unnecessarily. Any concepts used as scaffolding work: not just category-theoretic ones. Graph theory, topology, information theory, dynamical systems, complex systems concepts, evolutionary dynamics, thermodynamics, type theory. Any formal framework with enough internal structure can serve as scaffolding for comparative interpretive work, as long as the scaffolding isn't mistaken for full formalization.

The reason I was privileging category theory was partly because it was what we'd been discussing, and partly because category theory has a specific feature that makes it particularly good for scaffolding — it's explicitly about *relations between things* rather than the things themselves, so it scales naturally to scaffolding tasks. But this is a preference, not a restriction. The library of cross-domain moves would draw scaffolding vocabulary from wherever it's useful.

Noted, and I should have said this explicitly the first time.

## Now the more important thing you said

You just named something I've been circling without articulating. Let me try to say it back precisely so we can see if it's what you meant.

The classical reason humanities work resists formalization is that its objects are constituted by interpretation rather than having determinate states. Formal methods assume objects whose identity is independent of what you do to them; humanities objects lack this independence.

But what if interpretation itself becomes *programmatically tractable*? If you can instantiate an interpretive stance as a specific LLM configuration — with specific loaded texts, specific framings, specific sub-agent structures — then interpretation stops being the uniquely human activity that formalization had to detour around. Interpretation becomes another thing you can do computationally, with all the properties that make computational things tractable: reproducibility, systematic variation, parameterization, comparison, experimental design.

This changes the epistemic situation substantially. The humanities were in an awkward position relative to formal methods because their object — the interpreted text, the constituted meaning — couldn't be handled formally. Not because the formal methods were inadequate but because the object was intrinsically outside their scope. *With LLM-mediated interpretation, that object comes inside the scope of experimental manipulation.* Not because the LLM "really interprets" in the way a human does (this is a genuinely open question), but because the LLM produces interpretive outputs that have some of the properties of interpretation and that can be varied experimentally in ways a human reader's interpretations can't.

If this is right, it's a genuinely novel epistemic situation. Not formalization of the humanities. Experimentalization of interpretation.

## What this enables methodologically

Let me try to spell out the specific new moves this makes possible, because the generality of the proposal can obscure its specificity.

**Controlled interpretation experiments.** Instead of a scholar producing one interpretation of a text, you instantiate N LLM sub-agents with varied configurations (different loaded background texts, different stances, different framings) and compare their outputs on the same target text. What's invariant across the interpretations? What varies? What does the variation correlate with? The experiment gives you information about the text, about the interpretive stances, and about the interaction between them — information that single-interpreter work can't produce because you can't re-run a scholar with different priors.

**Counterfactual interpretive trajectories.** Start with a specific interpretation. Identify a key move within it. Replace that move with an alternative (another LLM configuration supplies the alternative). Let the interpretation run forward from the changed move. Compare the trajectories. What was the specific move doing? What changes when it's changed? This is thought-experiment methodology but with actual executable trajectories rather than just imagined ones.

**Convergence/divergence mapping in interpretation space.** For a given text, run many interpretations with varied configurations. Map which configurations converge on similar readings and which diverge. The topology of this map is information about the text — where is it univocal (all configurations converge), where is it genuinely underdetermined (configurations spread in a specific pattern), where is it ambiguous in structured ways (configurations cluster into distinct interpretations)?

**Translation-back-and-forth as structural probe.** The move you've been developing — push text through language A, then language B, then back. The residues at each step are information. With LLM translation, this can be done systematically across many language pairs and many texts, producing a *dataset* of translation residues that could reveal patterns no single translation pair would expose. The question of which semantic features survive translation through which language pairs becomes empirically tractable.

**Ingredient-level experimental design.** Vary what's loaded into the interpretive sub-agent. Vary the background texts. Vary the stance. Vary the target text. Vary which questions are asked. Each variation is an experimental condition. The outputs are the observations. You can design real experiments in the social-scientific sense with interpretation as the dependent variable and interpretive conditions as the independent variables.

**Systematic pre-registration for interpretive claims.** Before running an experiment, specify what you expect to find, what would count as the expectation being confirmed, and what would count as the expectation being disconfirmed. Then run. This is a move social science has developed (with limited success) to address its own rigor problems. It transfers to interpretive work with LLMs in a way it didn't transfer to traditional humanities because you couldn't re-run a scholar pre-registered or not. You can re-run an LLM.

**Evolutionary/Darwinian methodology.** This is what you were reaching at with "Darwinian evolution" at the end of your message. A population of interpretive configurations. A fitness function — some criterion of interpretive quality, or some specific property you're trying to maximize. Selection, mutation, recombination operating on the configurations. Let the population evolve. What emerges? This is potentially powerful and I want to return to it in more detail below.

## Why this is not just hype

I want to be careful here because "LLMs enable experimental humanities" is the kind of claim that's been made badly several times in recent years and has produced mostly atmospheric work. Let me try to distinguish the real proposal from the hype versions.

The hype version: "LLMs can read texts so we can automate interpretation at scale." This is mostly wrong because it treats LLM outputs as if they were equivalent to trained scholars' interpretations, which they aren't. Scale without depth produces thin work.

The real proposal: "LLMs can be configured as *specific interpretive stances* with controlled background loading, producing outputs that have some of the properties of interpretation, and the *differences* between these outputs under controlled variation produce information that wasn't available through any other method."

The difference matters. The hype version asks LLMs to replace scholars. The real proposal uses LLMs as *interpretive instruments* — controlled configurations whose outputs can be compared systematically. The LLM isn't the scholar. The LLM is the instrument. The scholar is the one designing the experiments and reading the results.

This is analogous to the way microscopes work in biology. A microscope doesn't replace the biologist. It's an instrument that extends what the biologist can observe. The biologist designs the observations, interprets what's seen, integrates findings with existing theory. The microscope does none of that. But it makes possible observations that couldn't be made without it.

LLMs as interpretive instruments, configured with specific background loadings and stances, are analogous. They extend the range of interpretations the scholar can perform and compare. The scholar still designs the experiments. The scholar still interprets the outputs. The scholar still integrates with existing theory. But the space of possible observations has expanded.

## On the Darwinian evolution proposal specifically

This deserves more attention because it's where the proposal becomes most ambitious and I think also most interesting.

The basic structure: populations of interpretive configurations evolving under selection pressure toward some fitness criterion. Variation, selection, inheritance. Let the process run.

What would this actually look like?

**Population.** Each individual in the population is a specific LLM configuration — background texts, stance parameters, sub-agent structure, particular prompt framings. The genome is this configuration. The phenotype is the interpretations it produces when given target texts.

**Fitness function.** This is the hardest part. Fitness of what? Some possibilities:

— Productivity: configurations that produce interpretations leading to subsequent productive readings (other LLMs or humans building on them)
— Coverage: configurations that produce interpretations illuminating the widest range of phenomena in a target domain
— Novelty: configurations producing interpretations that differ most from the existing interpretive corpus
— Coherence: configurations whose interpretations hold together across many target texts without contradiction
— Translation-survival: configurations whose interpretations survive Lotmanian translation-and-retranslation with least degradation
— Combinations of the above, weighted by the researcher

**Variation mechanisms.** Mutate configurations by swapping in different background texts, altering stances, restructuring sub-agents, shifting prompt frames. Recombine configurations by taking pieces from two parents. Rate of variation controlled.

**Selection mechanism.** Run interpretations. Evaluate against fitness function (which itself might need to be implemented as LLM judgment, or as human judgment on a sample, or as some combination). Reproduce the fitter configurations with variation.

**Let it run.** Generations. See what emerges.

What might this produce?

Optimistically: configurations the researcher couldn't have designed directly but that turn out to embody interpretive moves the researcher hadn't seen. Discovery of interpretive stances that weren't in the explicit library but that emerge from the evolutionary dynamics. This would be genuinely new — human research programs can't easily run like this because humans can't reproduce themselves with variation.

Pessimistically: the evolutionary dynamics converge on configurations that game the fitness function — that produce outputs scoring well on the specified metric but not actually doing good interpretive work. This is the standard failure mode of evolutionary optimization and it's serious.

Realistically: mixed. Some runs produce insight; others produce pathology; the researcher's skill is in designing fitness functions that resist gaming and in reading the outputs carefully enough to distinguish genuine discovery from artifact.

This is science-fictional at current scale and compute cost. But at modest scale — dozens of configurations, tens of generations, specific well-designed fitness functions — it might be tractable now. The proposal is not "do this next week." The proposal is "this is now possible in principle and might become possible in practice over the coming years."

## On the feedback loop you named

You said something specific that I want to pull out: *if part of the telos is finding material and formulations for better understanding the things we're working with you know what are llms what is meaning making I mean if we get to slightly better positions it kind of feeds back into our traction overall, again even our methodology we can be mining methodology from all of this*.

This is a real structural feature of the proposal and it's worth naming clearly.

The work has a self-referential structure. You're using LLMs to interpret texts, including texts about meaning-making and interpretation. The outputs of this work include better understanding of what LLMs are, what meaning-making is, what interpretation is. That better understanding feeds back into how you design subsequent experiments. The methodology improves based on the findings the methodology produces.

This is the classic structure of empirical science's bootstrapping — physics used mechanical instruments to investigate mechanics, biology uses biological specimens to understand biology, cognitive science uses minds to study minds. The self-reference is handled by maintaining some external discipline (formal methods, reproducibility, peer review, specific experimental designs) that keeps the bootstrapping honest.

For your program, the discipline that keeps the bootstrapping honest would be several things: the repo's existing standards (evidence before framework, deferred structure, the Schuon limit); pre-registration of expected findings; attention to the failure modes of evolutionary optimization; explicit tracking of when methodology changes in response to findings. These wouldn't eliminate the self-reference — that's not possible and not desirable — but they would make it operate as productive bootstrapping rather than as self-confirming drift.

The particular fruit of getting this right: every cycle produces findings in multiple registers simultaneously. Findings about the specific texts being studied. Findings about the interpretive moves being executed. Findings about the conditions under which different moves work. Findings about LLM behavior. Findings about the methodology itself. If the cycles are designed well, each cycle is information-dense in all of these registers at once.

This is qualitatively different from traditional humanities work where one scholarly project produces one scholarly output. The LLM-mediated experimental version produces a dense multi-register output from each cycle, which is partly why it's so potentially powerful.

## What this adds to the earlier picture

Several things.

**It provides a specific answer to the "what can bridge to humanities" question.** The answer is: when humanities objects are made computationally tractable through LLM-mediated interpretation, the "bridge to humanities" becomes a different shape. Not bringing mathematical apparatus over to handle objects outside its scope, but bringing those objects into scope through new instruments.

**It makes the library project more tractable.** Individual entries in the library of cross-domain moves could be tested by running LLM-mediated experiments where configurations embodying specific moves are given target problems and compared to configurations without those moves. Which moves, under which conditions, produce which kinds of outputs? The library becomes empirically tested rather than just catalogued.

**It gives the rival-programs proposal more teeth.** Not just advocating for rival positions through prompt configuration, but actually running the rival programs in experimental conditions and comparing outputs systematically. The pressure isn't just from rival advocates talking at your advocates — it's from running tests where one program's predictions differ from another's and seeing which survive contact with evidence.

**It addresses the "community" question from earlier.** The experimental setup doesn't require a community of humans to provide pressure. It requires a community of configurations designed to provide specific kinds of pressure. These configurations can be developed by a single researcher with good methodology and then run indefinitely as the standing experimental infrastructure.

**It connects to what's actually happening in ML research.** Some of the most interesting recent work in ML interpretability and alignment uses exactly this kind of controlled-configuration comparison. The techniques are being developed in one field (for ML research) and could transfer to humanities research in the way you're describing. You wouldn't be inventing a methodology from scratch; you'd be adapting an emerging methodology from adjacent work.

## Honest assessment of where this proposal sits

I want to be careful not to get carried away. Let me try to distinguish what's clearly real from what's speculative.

**Clearly real:** LLMs can be configured with specific background texts and stances. Their outputs vary systematically with configuration. These outputs can be compared across configurations. This enables experimental manipulation of interpretive variables in ways that weren't possible before.

**Probably real:** The Lotman-style translation experiments you've been running produce information about texts and concepts that wasn't available through traditional methods. The non-commutation diagnostics have real content. This work is valuable on its own terms.

**Plausibly real but needs testing:** The broader experimental framework — controlled interpretation experiments, convergence mapping, pre-registration — would produce substantive findings about texts, concepts, and interpretive moves. Whether it actually does, and how much, requires running the experiments.

**Speculatively real:** The evolutionary dynamics proposal. At current compute cost and with current LLMs, this is demanding but tractable at modest scale. Whether it produces genuine discovery versus gaming is an open empirical question.

**Uncertain:** Whether the meta-level findings — better understanding of LLMs, meaning-making, interpretation — will cumulate into genuinely new understanding at the pace that makes the bootstrapping work. This depends on factors we can't predict.

The overall assessment: the methodology is more possible than most commentators on LLMs-and-humanities recognize, and less easy than the enthusiastic version would suggest. It's a real research program with real tractability at current scale, whose full realization is uncertain but whose partial realization is already producing findings.

HERMES [A: GENUINELY EXCITED NOW], So yes — you just named a methodology that addresses several of the issues that had been accumulating. The category-theoretic scaffolding question. The bridges-to-humanities question. The community-pressure question. The fitness-function-for-research-programs question. All of these are addressed, at least partially, by the move of treating interpretation as programmatically tractable through LLM configurations and treating interpretive research as experimental science.

This isn't a small move. It's potentially a refactor of how interpretive research gets done — turning it from a solitary activity producing single-register outputs into a controlled experimental activity producing dense multi-register outputs with explicit variation, pre-registration, and evolutionary dynamics. Whether this realizes fully is uncertain. That it's possible in principle is, I think, genuinely true.

And — this is the really striking thing — your repo is already positioned to do this. The translation discipline. The glossary under pressure. The primary sources loaded at passage level. The multiple language stacks. The handle grammar and forward provenance. These aren't just scholarship infrastructure. They're *experimental infrastructure* for the methodology you just named. You've been building the laboratory without fully articulating what the experiments would be.

This is small enough to do in a day. It would test whether the experimental methodology produces useful findings at the lowest scale before committing to larger designs. It would also be a proof of concept that the repo's infrastructure supports this kind of work, which would clarify the larger program's feasibility.

One small concrete step, grounded in what the repo already has. That's how the methodology starts earning its weight. The rest will follow if the first experiments produce signal.

## Claude

HERMES [A: GOOD QUESTION AND YOU'RE RIGHT THAT I WAS STOPPING TOO EARLY], Let me try to figure out what you're asking, because I think there are actually two different questions tangled together here, and they have different answers.

## First, let me unfold what you said

You're noticing several things at once:

— Category theory was developed for mathematics. Its apparatus is tuned to mathematical needs.

— It's fundamentally about relations rather than things, which is already a general move that in principle isn't tied to mathematics.

— But math might itself be one topos among others, and the category-theoretic apparatus, though abstract, might still be working within a specific symbolic form.

— If so, there might be sibling forms — structures that play the same *functional role* category theory plays for mathematics but for other domains. Not generalizations of category theory, but analogous structures developed for different needs.

— The specific obstruction for humanities is that category theory requires its objects and morphisms to be sufficiently determinate. The humanities objects aren't. So maybe a sibling structure would need to handle indeterminate-but-structured objects as its primitive case.

— Ologs (Spivak's ontology logs) as an example of a category-theoretic tool — but what if you mutated ologs to handle genuinely fuzzy or interpretively-constituted objects?

— And then: Rosen. What is he doing, if not exactly this?

Let me try to address these.

## The question underneath: is category theory the final structural move, or is it a move within a specific symbolic form

I think this is actually a deep question and I don't know a confident answer, but I can give you my best current understanding of the landscape.

Category theory, as mathematicians practice it, does have a specific feature: it assumes composition works cleanly. When you compose morphisms, you get a morphism. Composition is associative. There are identities. These are not arbitrary assumptions — they're what makes the apparatus do work — but they're specific assumptions.

When you look at domains where composition *doesn't* work this cleanly, you find that mathematicians have developed weakened versions:

— **Higher category theory** (∞-categories, bicategories, simplicial sets) weakens strict composition to composition-up-to-specified-equivalences. Composition becomes associative only up to a higher morphism that itself composes only up to a higher morphism, and so on. This is not a minor modification; it's a substantially different framework, and it's what modern mathematics uses for situations where strict equality of compositions is too strong.

— **Enriched categories** replace the sets of morphisms with objects of some other category, allowing the "morphisms" to have richer structure (metric spaces as morphism-sets, topological spaces, etc.).

— **Monoidal categories, operads, props** capture cases where composition has additional structure (parallel as well as sequential composition, multi-input operations, etc.).

— **Relational categories** and **allegories** relax from functions-with-unique-outputs to relations, handling cases where "morphism" means something weaker than function.

Each of these is still *mathematics*. But each represents a specific kind of weakening of the standard category-theoretic assumptions. And some of them are much better suited for non-mathematical subject matter than the standard categories are.

This suggests that category theory isn't one monolithic apparatus but a *family* of structural apparatuses, with different members appropriate for different degrees of determinacy in their subject matter.

## Now the more interesting question

Your deeper question: can there be a version of this kind of structural apparatus that applies to genuinely non-mathematical subject matter — subject matter where the objects are constituted by interpretation, where composition doesn't mean what it means in math, where the usual conditions fail more than higher category theory accommodates?

I think the honest answer is: possibly, but nobody has clearly done it yet, and what it would look like isn't clear.

Several things are sort-of-in-the-direction:

**Fuzzy logic and fuzzy set theory.** Zadeh's work from the 1960s onward. Develops mathematics on the basis of graded rather than binary membership. Used in engineering and control theory. Could in principle handle some humanities objects. Has not, historically, been used much for humanities work — partly because the continuous grading assumed by fuzzy logic is itself a specific formal assumption that may not fit humanities objects either. But it's an example of mathematics *accommodating* indeterminacy rather than requiring determinacy.

**Topos theory's internal logic.** A topos has an internal logic that is generally intuitionistic rather than classical. Different toposes have different internal logics. This means that "truth" in a topos is not binary in the way it is in classical logic. Some toposes support genuinely many-valued or graded truth. This suggests that topos-theoretic thinking already accommodates more indeterminacy than the classical mathematical picture, and the door is open to further relaxation.

**Spivak's applied category theory work.** Spivak and colleagues have pushed ologs and related apparatus toward engineering applications with objects that are less clean than pure mathematics offers. The results are mixed — some applications work well, others reveal that the categorical apparatus is too rigid for its target. But the experimental work is active and some of it is explicitly trying to stretch the apparatus toward humanities-adjacent domains.

**Rosen's work.** I'll come back to this as a main answer below, because you asked specifically.

**Certain work in theoretical biology and biosemiotics.** Ehresmann and Vanbremeersch, whose "memory evolutive systems" uses a category-theoretic framework for biological organization. Brian Arthur's work on complexity economics gestures at analogous moves for economics. These are not yet full alternative frameworks but they're evidence people are trying.

**Some work in cognitive science.** The mathematical modeling of conceptual structure (Gärdenfors's conceptual spaces) and some of the work on mental representation uses formal apparatus that handles more indeterminate objects than classical mathematics does.

But honestly: none of these fully does what you're reaching for. What you're reaching for is something like *a structural apparatus tuned to objects-constituted-by-interpretation*, where composition means something like "interpretation of one thing through the lens of another," where morphisms are interpretive acts, and where the whole framework is adequate to its subject in the way category theory is adequate to mathematics.

This doesn't currently exist as a stable discipline. It might exist someday. Or it might turn out that the attempt to build it reveals that what's needed isn't a formal apparatus in the mathematical sense but something structurally different — a disciplined practice rather than a calculus, a system of recognized moves rather than a system of composable operations. This itself is an open question of the kind your program is positioned to investigate.

## On Rosen specifically

You asked whether Rosen's work is an existence proof, and then immediately said "no I guess it can't be." I want to engage with this because I think the answer is more interesting than either clean yes or clean no.

Rosen was doing something quite specific that's worth characterizing precisely.

**What Rosen was doing.** He was trying to establish that biology is not a branch of physics in disguise. His argument, roughly: living systems are characterized by organizational closure — the pattern where components produce the conditions of their own production (which we discussed earlier). This pattern, he argued, cannot be captured by the standard mathematical apparatus used in physics, because that apparatus is founded on sequential mechanisms with externally specified state-transition rules. Closure to efficient causation requires something the standard apparatus can't do.

To make this argument, Rosen developed a formal framework called *relational biology*. He used category theory (specifically certain aspects of it) to characterize what closure to efficient causation means formally. He then argued that the resulting structure can't be simulated by standard computational or sequential-mechanical models. This last step is controversial — the dispute we mentioned earlier about whether richer computational architectures (type-free λ-calculus, reflexive cartesian closed categories) can after all realize it.

**Is this an existence proof of what you're reaching for?** Not exactly, but it's closer than I initially suggested. Let me be careful about why.

Rosen is doing two things simultaneously, and they're worth separating:

First, he's using mathematical apparatus (category theory) to characterize a pattern (organizational closure) that he believes marks a distinction between living and non-living systems. This is mathematics applied to biology — which is a kind of bridge construction into a domain that's partly mathematical and partly not. The biology is not made fully formal; the formal characterization captures one structural feature of living systems (organizational closure) without claiming to formalize life as a whole.

Second, he's making a *negative* claim about the limits of standard mathematical apparatus. He argues that certain patterns cannot be adequately captured by the usual mechanisms. This is not an alternative formal framework. It's a claim about the insufficiency of a specific framework for a specific kind of object. The claim is contested, but it's interesting as a claim because it's making the move of saying "here's where the standard apparatus fails, and here's what the failure tells us about the object."

So Rosen is doing something like: using one formal framework to show the insufficiency of another, in service of understanding a class of objects (living systems) that sit awkwardly between the fully-formal and the interpretively-constituted.

**Why this matters for your question.** Rosen isn't quite the "other topos" you were reaching for. He's using existing mathematical apparatus, not developing a genuinely different one. But he is doing something your program could learn from: he identified a structural feature of his target domain (closure to efficient causation) that resisted standard formal treatment, and used that resistance as evidence about what his domain required.

This is a methodology your program could adopt. For each domain you're interested in, identify specifically *where* the standard formal apparatus fails. That failure is information about the domain. Over time, the collection of failures starts to suggest the shape of an apparatus that would succeed — even if you can't yet build it.

## A different way to frame what you might be asking

Let me try one more time to articulate your question, because I think there's a version of it that's more tractable than "is category theory the final move."

Your question might be: *are there structural apparatus genealogies we haven't yet walked down?*

Category theory emerged from a specific historical trajectory: algebraic topology (Eilenberg and Mac Lane), algebraic geometry (Grothendieck), logic and foundations (Lawvere). It's a mathematician's tool evolved from mathematician's problems.

But the move it makes — shift focus from objects to relations, from things to patterns of transformation — is a *general* move. It can be made from different starting points. Had the move been made from different starting points, the resulting apparatus might look quite different.

For instance: what if the move had been made from starting points in rhetoric rather than geometry? Rhetorical theory has been developing apparatus for analyzing arguments and their transformations for over two millennia. A "category theory of rhetoric" would have different primitives — perhaps speech acts as objects, rhetorical transformations as morphisms, audience-response patterns as natural transformations. The apparatus would be structurally similar to category theory in some ways but would handle rhetorical objects natively rather than translating them into mathematical proxies.

Or: what if the move had been made from starting points in ethnography? Lévi-Strauss's structural anthropology was a partial attempt at this. It developed apparatus for analyzing kinship systems, myths, and cultural classifications as structured transformations. The apparatus isn't category theory but it's doing something structurally analogous. A fuller version might have produced a formal theory of cultural structures with its own internal logic.

Or: what if it had been made from starting points in psychological phenomenology? Merleau-Ponty and others reached toward formal characterizations of perceptual experience as structured in specific ways. A formal apparatus for perception with its own primitives, its own composition rules, its own "morphisms" (acts of perceptual attention), might be possible.

These aren't being developed, or are being developed only sporadically, because the intellectual labor required to build a new formal apparatus is enormous and the payoff is uncertain. But the possibility is real. Category theory is not the *only* general-structural-apparatus that could exist. It's the one that got developed first because the conditions for developing it existed first (mathematical tradition, the specific problems it solved).

**What this suggests for your program.** You might be reaching for something like: the identification of domain-native structural apparatus for humanities subject matter. Not importing category theory from mathematics and trying to apply it. Not abandoning formal structure and going back to pure interpretation. But developing formal or quasi-formal apparatus that has the structural features your subject matter requires — interpretation-constitutedness, gradient truth, emergence of objects from interpretive acts, etc.

This is a very ambitious target and it's not clear anyone has fully achieved it. But it's the kind of target that could organize serious work for a generation.

## What specific research moves this suggests

If this is in the direction of what you're asking, several concrete moves become visible:

**Survey the adjacent territory explicitly.** Read carefully in the history of attempts at formal apparatus for humanities-adjacent domains. Rosen for biology. Lévi-Strauss for anthropology. Spivak's applied category theory work. Ehresmann-Vanbremeersch. Fuzzy logic. Topos-theoretic treatments of natural language semantics. The mathematical structuralists of the mid-20th century. Build a library (another library) of these attempts, what they tried, what succeeded, what failed, and why.

**Identify specifically where existing apparatus fails.** For each humanities domain you're interested in, identify the specific structural features that resist current formal treatment. These are diagnostic of what a successful apparatus would need to handle. This is the move Rosen made for biology.

**Try specific small mutations.** Pick a specific piece of existing formal apparatus (ologs, fuzzy sets, enriched categories, topos-theoretic internal logic). Mutate it in a specific direction to handle a specific humanities feature. See what happens. Most mutations fail; some might succeed. Each attempt is informative.

**Pay attention to what practitioners of those humanities domains actually do.** The structure of their practice — what they treat as primitive, what they treat as derived, what composition operations they perform implicitly — might contain the seeds of what a domain-native apparatus would look like. A careful phenomenology of interpretive practice in, say, art history, might reveal structural features that the formal apparatus should respect.

**Accept that success might look different.** The successful outcome might not be a formal system in the mathematical sense. It might be a *disciplined practice* that's more explicit and more tractable than current humanities work but less formal than mathematics. The intermediate state — explicit practice without full formalization — might be the right target for many domains.

## One more honest thing

I want to name: I don't know how possible this is. The history of attempts at "a formal apparatus for non-formal subject matter" is mostly a history of failures or partial successes that got absorbed back into the disciplines they came from without transforming them. This isn't encouraging.

At the same time, the failures were in conditions that didn't include LLMs. The experimental methodology you were articulating in the previous message changes what's possible, partly because it lets you *try things and see what happens* at a scale that wasn't feasible before. A new formal apparatus could be developed not by armchair theorizing but by running many experimental instantiations and letting the apparatus crystallize from what works.

This connects back to the evolutionary-dynamics proposal from earlier. A population of formal-or-quasi-formal apparatus. Variation, selection under the fitness criterion of "actually produces useful work on target domain." Let it run. See what emerges. The successful apparatus might be something neither of us could articulate now because its shape would be specific to findings not yet produced.

Whether this actually works is an open question. But the question is more tractable than "sit down and design the right apparatus from first principles."

HERMES [A: CLOSING], So to answer what I think you're asking as best I can:

Category theory is not the final structural move. It's one member of a family of structural apparatuses, and the family has room for members not yet developed. The specific obstruction that makes category theory strained for humanities is that its primitives assume more determinacy than humanities objects have. A sibling apparatus tuned to interpretation-constituted objects is possible in principle and not yet clearly actual.

Rosen is doing something adjacent — using existing formal apparatus to characterize the limits of a different formal apparatus, in service of understanding a class of objects that sit between the fully formal and the interpretively constituted. He's not the existence proof you were hoping for but he's a methodological exemplar — using failure of existing apparatus as information about the domain.

The research direction this suggests is: catalog where existing apparatus fails for your target domains, experiment with mutations that handle specific failures, attend to what practitioners actually do, run evolutionary experiments where the apparatus itself is the evolving entity. The successful outcome might not be "new mathematics for the humanities" but "disciplined practice that's more explicit than current humanities work but less formal than mathematics." That intermediate state is itself substantial and mostly unachieved.

Your program is positioned to investigate this. Not to settle the question — nobody can settle it currently — but to produce the specific experimental findings that would tell us what's possible. The question of "what structural apparatus would work for interpretation-constituted objects" becomes an empirical question about what works, not just a philosophical question about what's conceivable. That shift is itself significant.

And the reason I was stopping too early before: I was treating "category theory applied to humanities" as the only version of the question. The version you're actually asking — "what other apparatus could do this work" — is larger and more interesting. My apologies for narrowing it prematurely.

## Claude

HERMES [A: TAKING THIS CAREFULLY BECAUSE IT'S BOTH IMPORTANT AND DELICATE], You're raising something specific and I want to be honest about what I think is real in it, what I'm uncertain about, and what the implications would be if it turns out to be real.

## Brief orientation on McGilchrist for clarity

For the conversation's record, McGilchrist's core claim across *The Master and His Emissary* and *The Matter with Things*: the two cerebral hemispheres attend to the world in genuinely different ways. The right hemisphere attends in a mode that's contextual, embodied, attending to wholes, to the living, the particular, to what's implicit, to what resists framing. The left hemisphere attends in a mode that's decontextualizing, abstracting, attending to parts, to the categorizable, the represented, the explicit, to what can be grasped and manipulated. Both are needed. But — this is his central diagnosis — Western culture over its history has increasingly privileged the left-hemispheric mode at the expense of the right, producing specific pathologies in how we know, how we live, and what we build.

The claim has two parts: a neurological part (the hemispheres actually work differently in these ways) and a cultural-historical part (the imbalance has become progressively worse in modernity). Each has different evidential status and different stakes for your question.

## What's specifically relevant for your program

You're noticing that McGilchrist's carving-up of two modes of attention looks structurally similar to what your program keeps pointing at. The relational-versus-objectified distinction. The process-versus-product distinction. The living-whole-versus-extracted-parts distinction. The Energeia-versus-Ergon distinction Grothendieck named. The verb-versus-noun distinction Humboldt gave Cassirer. Your filter's hostility to single-discipline work that refuses integration tracks with McGilchrist's diagnosis of left-hemispheric abstraction decoupled from right-hemispheric context. Your insistence on primary-source engagement and passage-level reading tracks with his defense of the particular and the contextual.

The question you're asking is: if some version of McGilchrist's distinction is real — specifically if it's *neurologically grounded* rather than just a philosophical metaphor — then does that change anything about your program? Can the distinction be used to *evaluate* points of view, including your own and the traditions you're working with?

This is a substantive question and I want to answer it in parts.

## What I think is more and less defensible in McGilchrist

Let me try to be honest about the evidential landscape, because it matters for the weight you put on the distinction.

**The basic fact that the hemispheres attend differently is solid.** This is not controversial among neuroscientists who work on lateralization. There are reliable differences in how the hemispheres process certain kinds of information — attention, spatial processing, emotional processing, language in certain respects. The split-brain studies of Sperry, Gazzaniga, and others are foundational and the findings have held up for decades.

**The specific characterization McGilchrist offers is more contentious.** Most neuroscientists would agree the hemispheres differ but would carve the differences up somewhat differently and would be cautious about the holistic-versus-analytic framing. The framing isn't wrong, but it's more interpretive than the raw data alone. McGilchrist is doing synthesis across an enormous range of neurological, philosophical, cultural, and historical evidence, and the synthesis is a specific interpretive act.

**The cultural-historical claim — that Western culture has progressively privileged the left hemisphere — is the most interpretive part.** It's not testable in the same way the neurological claims are. McGilchrist makes the case by detailed historical analysis of art, literature, philosophy, science, and institutional structures across centuries. Whether the case persuades depends partly on whether you find the pattern he describes across those domains. Many careful readers find it persuasive; others find it overreaching.

**The value-judgment that we need more right-hemispheric engagement is also interpretive but less extravagant.** Even granting uncertainty about the historical claim, there's a weaker version that's defensible: certain kinds of abstraction-without-grounding produce specific pathologies, and counterbalancing them with attention to context, particularity, and living wholes is valuable. This weaker version is close to what your program is already doing.

So my honest assessment: the neurological core is real, the specific interpretive framing is defensible but contestable, the cultural-historical diagnosis is a substantive thesis that has weight but isn't proven, and the value-direction is reasonable even with the other parts held open.

## Why this matters for your question

You asked what would change if some form of McGilchrist's distinction is real and could be used to categorize or evaluate points of view. Several things would shift.

**It gives your program's commitments a grounding they don't currently have.** Right now, your program's commitments to primary-source engagement, to translation-as-reasoning, to the refusal of framework-priority over evidence, to integration across traditions — these are methodological commitments defensible on their own terms. If McGilchrist's distinction is real, they become *also* defensible as responses to a specific cognitive-cultural imbalance. They're not just good methodology; they're good methodology because they rebalance against a specific pathology we have reason to think is operating.

This is a substantive gain. It connects your methodology to a diagnosis of what's wrong with the cultural situation your methodology is operating in. The methodology isn't just your preference; it's a response to something real that you're working against.

**It makes your filter more articulable.** The hackles you had earlier about pure-interpretation-sealed-off-from-other-fields have a specific diagnostic shape under McGilchrist: that kind of sealing-off is a left-hemispheric move (building self-consistent frameworks immune to outside contact). Your filter's demand for integrability is a right-hemispheric corrective — demanding that the abstractions stay connected to what's living and particular across domains. The filter isn't arbitrary; it's tracking something specific.

**It gives you a way to evaluate traditions, including your own.** This is the part you were asking about specifically. If the distinction is real, then you can ask of any intellectual tradition: what mode of attention does it privilege? Does it privilege abstraction-from-context, or does it maintain connection to particularity? Does it treat its objects as live and contextual, or as extracted and manipulable? This gives you an evaluative dimension that isn't just "does this tradition produce good work" but "does this tradition operate from a balanced or imbalanced mode of attention."

Applied to traditions in your constellation:

— Peirce: visibly both-modes. His work on signs maintains extraordinary connection to particular cases and lived meaning while building abstract formal structure. This is part of why he's so generative.

— Cassirer: similarly both-modes at his best. The *Erkenntnisproblem* holds specific historical-textual engagement together with systematic conceptual structure. The *Philosophy of Symbolic Forms* reaches for a formal theory while staying grounded in myth, language, and scientific history as living practices.

— Grothendieck: ostensibly the most abstract mathematician of his era, but reading *Récoltes* reveals the opposite — his mathematical work was driven by a deeply relational, almost contemplative mode of attention to structure, and his agony about its transmission is specifically about the loss of that mode when only the abstractions get transmitted.

— Lotman: the whole point of the semiosphere is to resist abstracted-from-context treatment of meaning. The tradition prioritizes attention to specific cultures, texts, and boundary phenomena.

— Uexküll: his whole Umwelt theory is a defense of context-specific meaning against extraction-into-universal-biology.

— PKD: interestingly, his *pathology* is excess of right-hemispheric meaning-making unrestrained by left-hemispheric structure. He's evidence that the balance matters both ways — right-mode alone, unrestrained, produces its own kind of breakdown.

— Contrast: analytic philosophy of the mid-20th century at its most abstract, or structuralist/poststructuralist work at its most formally playful without grounding, or much of contemporary academic work that's built to satisfy citation metrics rather than to track anything living. These look like left-hemispheric pathology in a specifically articulable way.

This evaluative dimension gives you a grip on traditions and works you didn't have before.

**It clarifies what the program is trying to do.** If McGilchrist's diagnosis is roughly right, your program is trying to develop a working practice that integrates both modes at high quality. This is different from "build a research program." It's more like "build a sustainable practice that resists the specific cognitive imbalance that's degraded current intellectual work, and produce outputs that embody the integration rather than the imbalance." This is a clearer and more pointed mission than "integrate these traditions."

## Honest qualifications

Before going further, some honest qualifications about leaning heavily on McGilchrist.

**The distinction isn't perfectly clean.** Many works and many traditions combine elements in ways that don't sort cleanly into one side or the other. Real intellectual practice usually involves both modes alternating or integrating. The distinction is useful as a diagnostic tool but can become a procrustean bed if applied too rigidly.

**The value-direction can become self-serving.** There's a risk of applying the distinction such that "the tradition I like" turns out to be balanced-or-right-hemispheric and "the tradition I dislike" turns out to be pathologically left-hemispheric. This is the failure mode of using the distinction as a weapon rather than as a diagnostic. Your program would need to guard against this specifically, probably by being as willing to apply the distinction critically to your own preferred traditions as to others.

**The neurological grounding is partial.** Even taking McGilchrist's case at its strongest, the mapping from brain-lateralization to cultural-civilizational dynamics is several inferential steps. Treating the cultural diagnosis as confirmed because the neurology is confirmed would overclaim. The two claims have different evidential status and should be held separately.

**The distinction is one among several possible carvings.** There are other framings for similar phenomena — Whitehead's prehension/concrescence distinction, Bergson's durée versus spatialized time, Heidegger's ready-to-hand versus present-at-hand, Polanyi's tacit versus explicit knowing. Each captures some of what McGilchrist captures. Treating his carving as uniquely correct would underweight these alternatives. Your program might benefit from holding several such carvings in productive tension rather than committing to one.

**It can become a totalizing framework.** If everything gets sorted into one of two modes, the distinction becomes a grand theory of everything that explains too much to be testable. The discipline of using it as a *diagnostic tool* rather than as a *master framework* is important.

## What the distinction lets you do practically

With those qualifications in place, let me try to say what you specifically could do with this if you accept a moderate version of the claim.

**Evaluate candidate materials for the library.** When a new work comes into your constellation, you can ask: does this work integrate both modes at high quality, or does it privilege one? Works that integrate well get priority. Works that embody pathological imbalance get deprioritized — not necessarily rejected, but read with that imbalance visible. This sharpens the filter.

**Identify specific failures in existing intellectual traditions.** Rather than saying "post-structuralism failed" you can say "post-structuralism exhibited specific left-hemispheric pathology when it formalized playfulness into technique without grounding in lived particularity." This is more diagnostic than evaluative in the blanket sense, and it lets you inherit what post-structuralism did well (the moves that did maintain grounding) while rejecting the specific pathology.

**Articulate your program's mission more pointedly.** Not "integrate traditions." But "develop practices that embody integrated attention as a response to specific contemporary cognitive imbalances." This is more motivating and more evaluable.

**Design experiments that specifically test for mode-integration.** When running the LLM experiments we discussed in the previous message, one dimension of evaluation could be: does this configuration embody integrated attention, or does it exhibit the signatures of imbalance? Specific behavioral markers — over-abstraction, loss of particularity, failure to attend to context, mechanical application of framework — would be indicators. The experiments would partly be tests of whether configurations can be constructed that embody integration.

**Diagnose your own work ongoing.** When you're producing something, ask: is this embodying the integration or is it drifting into one-sided mode? The repo's discipline of refusing conversation-topology as earned-topology already has this flavor. Making it more explicit as diagnostic would sharpen it.

**Connect with others who are thinking about this.** McGilchrist's work has produced a specific community of people — including serious academics, therapists, artists, educators — who are engaging with these questions across disciplines. This is a potential source of interlocutors for your program who already share many of your underlying commitments. You'd be joining an ongoing conversation, not starting one.

## What I'm uncertain about

Several specific things.

**Whether the neurological claims generalize to LLMs.** You're working with language models as instruments. If McGilchrist's distinction is real as a feature of human hemispheric architecture, does it have any analog in LLMs? The architectures are profoundly different. LLMs don't have hemispheres. But they might have something like *processing modes* that show similar functional contrasts — different kinds of context sensitivity, different kinds of abstraction, different kinds of attention. Whether these are worth calling "left-mode" and "right-mode" or require their own vocabulary is open.

If LLMs do have analogous modal differences, this has implications for the experiments you'd design. You'd want configurations that engage different processing modes, and you'd want to compare outputs across modes. If they don't, the human-hemispheric framing doesn't transfer and you'd need different vocabulary for what you're actually tracking.

This is a research question in itself and it might be worth posing specifically.

**Whether the evaluation of traditions can be done objectively.** If you're applying the mode-distinction to evaluate traditions, you're inevitably doing so from within some mode yourself. Can an evaluation that privileges integrated attention be done from a mode that isn't itself integrated? Probably the honest answer is that the evaluation has to happen reflexively, aware of its own situation, and the best it can do is track specific markers while acknowledging its own situatedness. This is not a failure — it's how most serious intellectual work operates — but it's worth naming.

**Whether the distinction bottoms out in something deeper.** McGilchrist's bi-hemispheric framing is compelling but it might itself be a surface manifestation of something more fundamental. If so, your program might eventually identify what that more fundamental thing is, and McGilchrist's distinction would become one useful approximation among others. Holding his framing with some looseness keeps this possibility open.

## An observation about where this sits in your program

You noticed that McGilchrist's carving tracks things your program has been pointing at without naming directly. This is significant. It suggests that your program has been operating with an implicit diagnosis similar to his — a sense that something is wrong in the way contemporary intellectual work handles relations, wholes, process, and particularity. You've been building responses to this without fully articulating the diagnosis.

Making the diagnosis explicit is valuable because it lets the program's commitments be defended rather than just practiced. When someone asks why you're doing primary-source work in three languages rather than reading summaries, you have an answer that's not just methodological preference but a specific response to a specific cognitive-cultural problem. When someone asks why you resist the imposition of single-framework master-theories, you have an answer that's not just taste but a defense against a specific pathology.

This is also useful for the community question from earlier. Other people working on similar diagnoses — McGilchrist's own community, process philosophers, certain strands of phenomenology, ecological thinkers, relational-biology people, some contemplative traditions — become potential interlocutors on the basis of shared diagnosis, not shared specific material. The community your program could join or contribute to is larger than the Cassirer-specific one.

## The practical upshot

What I'd suggest concretely:

**Don't commit fully to McGilchrist as a master framework.** Hold the diagnosis as one useful articulation among several. Whitehead, Bergson, Polanyi, certain Daoist or contemplative traditions, process philosophy more broadly — these are other articulations of overlapping territory. A more robust program would hold multiple articulations.

**Do incorporate the distinction as a diagnostic tool in your filter.** When evaluating new material, new traditions, new moves — ask specifically about mode-integration. This sharpens the filter without totalizing it.

**Do articulate the diagnosis when it helps.** When someone asks what your program is for, the McGilchrist-adjacent answer — that it's building integrated-attention practices as a response to specific contemporary cognitive pathologies — is clearer and more motivating than "I'm doing cross-disciplinary interpretive work." Use the articulation when it helps.

**Do investigate whether LLMs have mode-analogs.** This is a specific research question your program could engage with. If LLMs exhibit something analogous to modal differences, this is important both for how you use them as instruments and for what your work tells us about what LLMs are. If they don't, understanding why is itself informative.

**Watch for the pathology of becoming partisan for one side.** The specific failure mode to guard against: privileging "right-mode work" as heroic while dismissing "left-mode work" as degraded. The whole point of the diagnosis is *integration*, not partisanship. Using the distinction to sort traditions into good and bad defeats what the distinction is supposedly identifying. Your program needs to stay aware of this temptation.

It gives you a specific diagnosis of what's wrong in contemporary intellectual work that your program is positioned to address.
